  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_7c322792-794d-46e3-814c-347dafd75411'

<div id="review_1710521795" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1710521795" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1710521795?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1710521795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11979450323923314733">Orson Scott Card’s very entertaining 1996 novel Pastwatch: The Redemption of Christopher Columbus is a time travel book and so much more.<br/><br/>Many great science fiction / fantasy writers have had fun and great success with time travel as an extension of their speculative vision. Heinlein, Poul Anderson, Bradbury, de Camp, H.G. Wells, Vonnegut, Twain, and Piers Anthony to name just a few. There seems to be as many approaches to the time travel conundrum as there are writers, but generally falling into</span>
  <span id="freeText11979450323923314733" style="display:none">Orson Scott Card’s very entertaining 1996 novel Pastwatch: The Redemption of Christopher Columbus is a time travel book and so much more.<br><br>Many great science fiction / fantasy writers have had fun and great success with time travel as an extension of their speculative vision. Heinlein, Poul Anderson, Bradbury, de Camp, H.G. Wells, Vonnegut, Twain, and Piers Anthony to name just a few. There seems to be as many approaches to the time travel conundrum as there are writers, but generally falling into one of two camps. There is the classic paradox scenario where a time traveller actually goes back in time and is a part of the action and so perhaps changes his own destiny. There is also the time traveller as voyeur, where the agent can only view and report. <br><br>This is a little of both.<br><br>Setting up a time travel process whereby scientists can “see” into the past, the sightseers make an astonishing discovery that perhaps they can be seen and influence those in the past. From here comes the next step of travel, and so Card is off.<br><br>The subject is good ole Christopher Columbus and his world-changing voyage. Should he have gone east instead of west to influence the Crusades? What would that be like? Could travellers making influential changes create a worse result? Card asks and answers many of these questions and creates a fecundity of time travel paradoxical theorizing.<br><br>Columbus is more than just a time traveller’s target, Card spends plenty of time getting to know the Genoese and this history seems well researched and deftly produced.<br><br>The reader is thus entranced and entertained, spell bound by Card’s exceptional storytelling and invited to consider a myriad of time travel what ifs.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1514422263i/24772505.jpg" width="400" height="319" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="11979450323923314733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19850991" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19850991" />
    <a title="Rhyd Wildermuth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1003139-rhyd-wildermuth"><img alt="Rhyd Wildermuth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206036629p2/1003139.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19850991?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rhyd Wildermuth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rhyd Wildermuth" href="/user/show/1003139-rhyd-wildermuth">Rhyd Wildermuth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Homophobes, Fundamentalists, Misogynists, White Supremacists, and Mormons </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19850991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer123692219685989567">No one can begrudge Card for using Sci-Fi as a field for propaganda: the medium itself (world-creation/world-defining) by nature almost requires it. <br/><br/>But unless you're rather fond of the idea that mormon "family values" are somehow universal, and extend throughout the whole history of humanity, than you might not go for this book. <br/><br/>I didn't. <br/><br/>If you're the sort who watches the history channel and finds it profound, somehow missing the propaganda within a narrative of human actions throughout record</span>
  <span id="freeText123692219685989567" style="display:none">No one can begrudge Card for using Sci-Fi as a field for propaganda: the medium itself (world-creation/world-defining) by nature almost requires it. <br /><br />But unless you're rather fond of the idea that mormon "family values" are somehow universal, and extend throughout the whole history of humanity, than you might not go for this book. <br /><br />I didn't. <br /><br />If you're the sort who watches the history channel and finds it profound, somehow missing the propaganda within a narrative of human actions throughout recorded time which asserts a long succession of wars to be an adequate measure of the passage of human activity, than perhaps you'll have no problem with Card's proposed defense for european colonialism (his thesis: it could have been worse--we could have let the natives handle things!). <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="123692219685989567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1112898276?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312850586" href="/book/show/478956.Pastwatch">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=sci-fi-time-travel">sci-fi-time-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1112898276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11981118837052875752">The next stop in my time travel marathon was <i>Pastwatch: The Redemption of Christopher Columbus</i>, the 1996 novel by Orson Scott Card. This was my introduction to Card, one of the more prolific science fiction authors working; his <i>Ender</i> saga alone equals the flex of most writers out there. <i>Pastwatch</i> was rumored to be the beginning of a series, and with the attention to both character and history, as well as dedication to a rousing good tale, I couldn't be more excited to visit this world again.<br/><br/><i>Past</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11981118837052875752" style="display:none">The next stop in my time travel marathon was <i>Pastwatch: The Redemption of Christopher Columbus</i>, the 1996 novel by Orson Scott Card. This was my introduction to Card, one of the more prolific science fiction authors working; his <i>Ender</i> saga alone equals the flex of most writers out there. <i>Pastwatch</i> was rumored to be the beginning of a series, and with the attention to both character and history, as well as dedication to a rousing good tale, I couldn't be more excited to visit this world again.<br /><br /><i>Pastwatch</i> begins at the dawn of the 23rd century. Mankind has stepped back from the brink of destruction to unite in the restoration of the earth's resources. Agriculture has eliminated hunger. Education has uplifted the minds and futures of the youth. Science has given the world the Tempoview, a machine that permits the members of Pastwatch to observe history. Pastwatch has no ability to alter the past, only to view it, study it, document lives that will prevent mankind from making the same mistakes over and over again.<br /><br />Tagiri is a historian who has used to Tempoview to trace her ancestors back seven generations to a mountainous village in Africa. Tagiri begins to follow the lives of the village's women in reverse, from death to birth, a method that takes her from the misery of one of her subjects to its cause: the abduction of the woman's son by a slaver. Tagiri later makes an even more alarming discovery when one of her subjects relates a dream of being watched by a dark woman seven generations in the future. This indicates that Pastwatch might not be as neutral as believed.<br /><br />It is Tagiri's theory that the genocide of the New World began with the return of Christopher Columbus to Spain, where he made extravagant promises of gold and spices he had not actually found on his maiden voyage. Tagiri's colleague Hassan is promoted to what becomes known as The Columbus Project, a study to determine what exactly led Columbus to cross the Atlantic in the first place and whether his discovery might be stopped. Tagiri and Hassan marry and have a daughter, Diko, who grows up observing scenes of Columbus' life so often she comes to regard him as an uncle.<br /><br />The novel forks off to trace the rise of Columbus, son of a Genovese weaver, born without wealth or title. He watches his tradesman father disrespected by the gentleman class and becomes obsessed with the procurement of gold to alter his family's fortunes. Learning everything he can about marine travel, charts and navigation, Columbus achieves some notoriety for exhibiting bravery and surviving a pirate attack on a convoy to Flanders. He improves his social standing by taking as wife the daughter of the late governor of Porto Santo, the homely Felipa Moniz. Columbus neglects her and their son to focus on his true passion: achieving his destiny by crossing the Atlantic.<br /><br />Diko becomes so skilled at using the latest in Pastwatch technology, the TruSite II, that she locates the moment in history Columbus decided to cross the Atlantic. After being shipwrecked by the pirates, he experiences a prophetic vision in the form of the Holy Trinity. The spirit speaks to Columbus, instructing him to sail westward. <i>"There are great kingdoms there, rich in gold and powerful in armies. They have never heard the name of my Only Begotten, and they die unbaptized. It is my will that you carry salvation to them, and bring back the wealth of these lands."</i><br /><br />With Diko's alarming discovery, two additional researchers join the Columbus Project. Kemal is a Turkish meteorologist who used the Tempoview to study historic sea level rise; this led him to the Red Sea, where Kemal traced the origins of both the Noah myth and the legend of Atlantis. He begins studying the rise of civilization in Mesoamerica when he learns of Tagiri's research into stopping slavery. Hunahpu is a Mayan historian whose thesis that a great civilization was on the rise in the Caribbean -- until the arrival of Columbus -- proves unpopular with his colleagues, except for Diko.<br /><br />She believes that Columbus' prophetic vision and his obsession with sailing west was hardly divine but the work of some alternate version of Pastwatch, which reached back in time and tampered with history, perhaps to avert the conquest of Europe by the bloodthirsty tribes of Mesoamerica. While this might have spared war in Europe, the alternate history enslaved the indigenous people of the New World, a historical evil that The Columbus Project seeks to atone for. While Columbus campaigns the court of Queen Isabella for a charter across the Atlantic, Diko, Hunahpu and Kemal volunteer to be sent back to stop him from succeeding, at least, succeeding as history recorded it. As a result, they will destroy the present and everyone left behind in it.<br /><br /><i>Pastwatch</i> is science fiction first and foremost, historical fiction second and maybe thriller much, much further down the line. In spite of this, I was enthralled by the novel and once the time travelers meet Columbus and begin alternating history, our history, I couldn't flip the pages fast enough. <br /><br />The novel started off fuzzy for me. I found it difficult to get a handle on exactly what was happening in the 23rd century, who all these characters with the strange names were. Even the conceit of Pastwatch -- with monitors that "see" into the past -- seemed sketchy to me.<br /><br />What makes the novel so spellbinding are the characters. Card does an outstanding job of populating his future trek with compelling human beings, scientists cut with passion and intelligence that I was able to relate to throughout. It was refreshing to find two African women and a Mayan and Turk featured so prominently -- there's nary a white man around. I was also surprised by how compelling the historical scenes were. Without the benefit of tech and with the outcome already known by history, Card devotes just as much energy to the launch of the Nina, the Pinta and the Santa Maria, a mission of discovery that parallels the efforts of Pastwatch 700 years in the future.<br /><br />I felt that the mechanisms for time travel might've been explained better and that it took too long for the time travelers to physically arrive in the past, but I did enjoy the cutting back and forth between science and historical fiction. Rather than blasting off into the Delta Quadrant, the drama is intertwined in the genocide and injustices of the past. And the speculation that Card gives in to late to build a history where the "discovery" of the New World turned it much differently, and much more beneficial, for all interested parties, was a pleasure to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11981118837052875752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Apart from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7955.Empire' title='Empire by Orson Scott Card' rel='nofollow noopener'>Empire</a> which I did not like, I have found Card's novels to be consistently excellent and both <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7967.Speaker_for_the_Dead__Ender_s_Saga___2_' title='Speaker for the Dead (Ender's Saga, #2) by Orson Scott Card' rel='nofollow noopener'>Speaker for the Dead</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95573.Hart_s_Hope' title='Hart's Hope by Orson Scott Card' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hart's Hope</a> are on my list of "All Time Favorites" (with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/375802.Ender_s_Game__Ender_s_Saga___1_' title='Ender's Game (Ender's Saga, #1) by Orson Scott Card' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ender's Game</a> not far behind. <br/><br/>I was surprised to see that this novel was not among the list of nominees for any of the SF awards during its year of eligibility. It was certainly worthy of being recognized as one of the best of 1996. RECOMMENDED!! </span>
  <span id="freeText8889212832922213128" style="display:none">4.0 to 4.5 stars. Another superb novel by OSC. <div id="review_166245661" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166245661" />
    <a title="hayden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2995566-hayden"><img alt="hayden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598996671p2/2995566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166245661?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="hayden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="hayden" href="/user/show/2995566-hayden">hayden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=doesnt-deserve-publication">doesnt-deserve-publication</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=doesnt-deserve-the-goodreads-rating">doesnt-deserve-the-goodreads-rating</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=wish-i-could-unread-it">wish-i-could-unread-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=expect-the-worst">expect-the-worst</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=hate-with-a-passion">hate-with-a-passion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=they-cut-down-trees-for-this-sh-t">they-cut-down-trees-for-this-sh-t</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=%E2%98%85">★</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=not-even-worth-a-star">not-even-worth-a-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=plot-devices-gone-awry">plot-devices-gone-awry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2995566-hayden?shelf=deathly-long-descriptions">deathly-long-descriptions</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166245661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1728834938448465930">This book is in the key of C Minor, because it's just that tragic.<br/><br/><u>
  <b>Final Verdict: 0.0000000000000000000000000000000000000001 Stars</b>
</u><br/><br/>Sometimes, English teachers make horrible choices.<br/><i>Pastwatch</i> is no exception.<br/>It was a terrible, horrible, no-good, very bad book.<br/><br/><b>
  <u>Three Big Things that Epically Suck About <i>Pastwatch</i>, in No Particular Order</u>
</b><br/><b>The First: The Plot Holes</b><br/>In <i>Pastwatch</i>, the characters go back in time and try to prevent the people of the past from dying. In one scene of the book, Manjam (a </span>
  <span id="freeText1728834938448465930" style="display:none">This book is in the key of C Minor, because it's just that tragic.<br><br><u>
  <b>Final Verdict: 0.0000000000000000000000000000000000000001 Stars</b>
</u><br><br>Sometimes, English teachers make horrible choices.<br><i>Pastwatch</i> is no exception.<br>It was a terrible, horrible, no-good, very bad book.<br><br><b>
  <u>Three Big Things that Epically Suck About <i>Pastwatch</i>, in No Particular Order</u>
</b><br><b>The First: The Plot Holes</b><br>In <i>Pastwatch</i>, the characters go back in time and try to prevent the people of the past from dying. In one scene of the book, Manjam (a scientist) explains to them how the Pastwatching works, but this description is shoddy at best, leaving many readers (including myself) totally mindfucked. It leads me to wonder if Orson even knows what he's writing. If <i>you</i> don't even understand how your world works, how do you expect <i>us</i> Which leads me to wonder how this teaches us, as students, anything. Why would you, anon English teacher, give us this book to read? What will it teach us? I only learned one thing from this book: to cross Orson Scott Card off my reading list. (Also to avoid splitting infinitives -- but I didn't learn this from the book itself, only from the work based around it.)<br><br><b>The Second: The Plot Itself</b><br>It was <i>boring</i>. I don't know how I can make myself any more clear. I didn't read this book for enjoyment, but if I did(which I obviously wouldn't), I wouldn't have continued reading past the second chapter. One of the chapters (God only knows which one; they all blend together) was twenty pages, and it didn't serve any purpose. There was absolutely no value. The only book I've ever read that has been that bad was <i>Three Cups of Tea</i> (if you need an explanation of that one, you can read my review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172095471" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>). The only scene in the entire book that I even partially liked was the last two-and-a-half pages of chapter nine. That's literally it. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I also liked the ending when they were all dead! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>The Third: The Characters</b><br>The only way I can think of describing them is <i>devoid of all emotion</i>. The characters are considering traveling back in time to stop deaths from long ago, and they discover that doing so would <i>blink out their entire current existence</i>! The entire planet would be dead! <b>AND THEY'RE NOT EVEN GOING TO TELL ANYONE ABOUT IT!</b> What kind of heartless person wouldn't tell everybody about killing them all to create an alternate universe?! (<b>***Spoiler alert for the rest of the paragraph***</b>: They end up going back in time and not getting anyone else's consent, or even telling anyone. The emotionless a-holes.) When Tagiri pulls the switch to send them all back, she doesn't care that she's about to die! <b>She doesn't fucking care!</b> That's not bravery; it's heartlessness. Anyone with a heart would feel at least a pang of sorrow at leaving everyone. Nope! Not Tagiri, or Diko, or Hunahpu, or Kemal. Nobody. What the hell. Gah.<br><br>It didn't help that I had to do three <a href="http://www.adlit.org/strategies/22091/" rel="nofollow noopener">double entry journals</a> for every fifty pages of the book. That pretty much sucked.<br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><br><b>Original Thoughts</b><br><br>Oh, <i>boy,</i> am I excited to read this! I sense another Snark-a-thon (remember <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172095471" rel="nofollow noopener">my Three Cups of Tea review</a>?) coming along for this sure-to-be-fantastic novel!<br><br>Those teachers just have a habit of choosing the wrong books. I can already tell this is going to be absolutely horrible.<br><br>I hope you are excited for how much I'm going to tear this book apart.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380222758ra/341222.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><span>[image error]</span><br><br><b>Five Minutes Later</b><br><br>I'm feeling better about Pastwatch. It's the first work of modern-ish fiction we've read this year, albeit science fiction (which Mrs. Teacher actually admitted to hating the other day!), and at least it's not as long as Three Cups of Tea. <br><br><b>Thirty Seconds Later</b><br><br>Okay, never mind. This book hits 122k, which is longer than Twilight.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380349485ra/703479.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380222758ra/503420.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is gonna suck.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="1728834938448465930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_10964583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10964583" />
    <a title="Hugh Henry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/560458-hugh-henry"><img alt="Hugh Henry" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10964583?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hugh Henry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hugh Henry" href="/user/show/560458-hugh-henry">Hugh Henry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/560458-hugh-henry?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10964583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18067974900894974201">This is a well-written work of science fiction, as are all of Card's works. Like his <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7959.Rachel_and_Leah_Women_of_Genesis' title='Rachel and Leah Women of Genesis by Orson Scott Card' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rachel and Leah</a>, however, the characters of this book who pretend to be historical are not very accurate. I enjoy good fiction and exciting narratives. I dislike fiction masquerading as history or a work such as this blurring the lines between history and fiction so thoroughly that it is impossible to see where the imagination ends and facts begin.<br/> The idea that the voyage of Columbus changed the entire face of </span>
  <span id="freeText18067974900894974201" style="display:none">This is a well-written work of science fiction, as are all of Card's works. Like his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7959.Rachel_and_Leah_Women_of_Genesis" title="Rachel and Leah Women of Genesis by Orson Scott Card" rel="nofollow noopener">Rachel and Leah</a>, however, the characters of this book who pretend to be historical are not very accurate. I enjoy good fiction and exciting narratives. I dislike fiction masquerading as history or a work such as this blurring the lines between history and fiction so thoroughly that it is impossible to see where the imagination ends and facts begin.<br /> The idea that the voyage of Columbus changed the entire face of the earth forever, bringing on Western civilization as we know it, is debatable. Pedro Cabral accidentally landed in Brazil a short time later. Who can say absolutely that Cabot, Cartier and the other exporers would NEVER have ventured into the Atlantic without Columbus' tales of a land of plenty? Secondly, it is highly far-fetched for any to suppose that a final crusade to retake Constantinople was in the offing in 1492. The Great Schism was 400 years old and the Greek Orthodox people were not considered Christians.<br /> Even more dubious is Card's contention that the Native Americans were <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6288.The_Road" title="The Road by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">on the road</a> to huge technological discoveries and massive, centralized empires worthy of competing with European powers in ship-building and exploration. Columbus' voyage and the succeeding colonizings by European countries stopped this enormous Central-American empire from taking shape. <br /> I put the book down as soon as Card began expounding that Malthus was right: Western culture will lead us in the year 2100 to the total destruction of the Amazon rainforest, all the topsoil in the world, and the total destruction of all arable land in the world. The apocalyptic future in which massive wars have killed 90% of the world's people, but the world is still unable to feed itself is beyond ludicrous. Thomas Malthus could have written this book.<br /> A fanciful history and a Malthusian future build the setting of Pastwatch on false foundations. I give it two stars because it is at least well-written.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18067974900894974201" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_388251660" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388251660" />
    <a title="Alisi ☆ wants to read too many books ☆" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9323801-alisi-wants-to-read-too-many-books"><img alt="Alisi ☆ wants to read too many books ☆" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344452757p2/9323801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388251660?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alisi ☆ wants to read too many books ☆" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alisi ☆ wants to read too many books ☆" href="/user/show/9323801-alisi-wants-to-read-too-many-books">Alisi ☆ wants to read too many books ☆</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">This book is perfect for psychologically breaking a person. </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9323801-alisi-wants-to-read-too-many-books?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9323801-alisi-wants-to-read-too-many-books?shelf=rue-the-day-you-learned-to-read">rue-the-day-you-learned-to-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9323801-alisi-wants-to-read-too-many-books?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388251660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12229636260489325790">Before I start this, I just have to say I have this kind of funny reaction to Card's books. I may like or love a book/series but when I find one that I hate, I want to tear the heavens apart and demand Card pay for my mental illness that was caused by reading it.<br/><br/>I mean, I hate-hate-hate-<i>hate</i> with a passion that borders on religious fervor. There is no meh or disappointed or even mild hatred or annoyance.<br/><br/>This is because Card is a very good story teller. He's made a huge name for himself and this </span>
  <span id="freeText12229636260489325790" style="display:none">Before I start this, I just have to say I have this kind of funny reaction to Card's books. I may like or love a book/series but when I find one that I hate, I want to tear the heavens apart and demand Card pay for my mental illness that was caused by reading it.<br /><br />I mean, I hate-hate-hate-<i>hate</i> with a passion that borders on religious fervor. There is no meh or disappointed or even mild hatred or annoyance.<br /><br />This is because Card is a very good story teller. He's made a huge name for himself and this is why his bad books are terrible. He believes since he's a highly respected author that it's okay to break every god damn rule of writing (no, I don't me the grey area of rules or even a blending of good or bad to balance out.)<br /><br />It's like he makes a list of all the worst things a writer can do in a story and then goes at it.<br /><br />This book, Pastwatch, is perhaps the worst book I've EVER read. <br /><br />First off, this book is about a group of scientists from the future that make a kind of devise that allows them to look into the past. They can't change it but they'll research it and such. <br /><br />So, as they go along, one of the scientists is looking back through her genealogy (which is supposed not allowed? I'm not sure. I think my mind blocked it.) In this lab is a man who is researching the past too.<br /><br />Eventually, they find a way to actually go back in time. Their attempt was to stop the the massacres that followed Christopher Columbus's journey. They did and find out that a huge massacre happens in Europe. The blood thirsty savages totally made mountains of sacrificed people. So they change it back.<br /><br />The only good parts of this book is the stories from the past. That's the only time he's telling a story. <br /><br />Every where else you'll find the book to be a huge soapbox for his fake science. I mean, people literally have a ten or twenty page discussion on Card's whimsy science then the two will part. One will leave the lab and down the hall, that person will find another to bump into and thus have another ten or twenty page discussion on his science. This bumping into each other thing just be a good 90%. It goes ON and ON and ON and OOOOOOOOOOOOON! <br /><br />95% of this book is just info dumping. Dumping isn't the right word. Info vomiting is more like it. Card, I don't care about your personal science! You're obviously proud and, hey, more power to you! But spare the rest of us!<br /><br />And those two in the lab I mention? Yeah, even though they rarely interacted and barely spoke to each other (and there is absolutely NO expression of even mild romantic interest or friendship), one day you find they've gotten married and have retired, and now their two GROWN children (also a girl and a boy) are doing the <b>same exact research</b> and starting at the <b>exact same place</b> that the newly wed/ newly formed family/ newly empty nesters/ newly retired never to appear again scientists were at before they went poof! This is like an instant thing. It's baffling. I'm like WHAT? Did they speak two sentences to each other?<br /><br />The Christopher Columbus this is also incredibly racist. His supposition is that if Columbus hadn't got their and killed all the brown people, they would've come to Europe in a couple years (as he says the people were almost at the same point in their ship building -- just give it a couple years type thing) to kill all the white folk. He says this while completely ignoring the fact that Columbus totally wiped them out. A couple years? Really? So how then, if these natives were so terrifying, did Columbus massacre them?<br /><br />It was like the worst apologetic argument supporting the wide spread genocide the Europeans wrought on the new world. And using the innocent white folk as sacrifices? He didn't even bother researching that. They didn't just pick someone up off the street. They had special wars and such to get the sacrifices (eg, the Flower Wars.)<br /><br />If anyone else tried to submit this book for publication, they would've been blacklisted. <br /><br />I like Card. However, I don't search out his books anymore. This book and a handful of his other books have taught me how foolish to seek him out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12229636260489325790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_98028750" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98028750" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1018754-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1227715451p2/1018754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98028750?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/1018754-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98028750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13837363216797437934">This is an idea book, not a character book. In this book, Card is exploring the idea that a group of people would deliberately go back in time to alter events in such a way that human history would work out "better." The height of hubris, definitely, for any group of mortals to think they could predict future events accurately enough to know what to "improve." I think that Card is right that humanity would have to be in the brink of extinction before they would permit such an experiment.<br/><br/>There ar</span>
  <span id="freeText13837363216797437934" style="display:none">This is an idea book, not a character book. In this book, Card is exploring the idea that a group of people would deliberately go back in time to alter events in such a way that human history would work out "better." The height of hubris, definitely, for any group of mortals to think they could predict future events accurately enough to know what to "improve." I think that Card is right that humanity would have to be in the brink of extinction before they would permit such an experiment.<br /><br />There are lots of ideas in this book...global warming, geopolitics, time travel, the complicated and interrelated history of European and ancient American peoples....it's also an exploration of the life of Christopher Columbus, which is very interesting. Columbus himself is the only fully fleshed-out character in the novel. The rest of the characters exist solely as props to keep the plot moving...they have just enough detail to add a little bit of human interest to the very complicated story.<br /><br />I thought the book was fascinating. But don't read it if you have to have a character you love in order to really enjoy a novel. You won't find that here. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13837363216797437934" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_52491810" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52491810" />
    <a title="Ellen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/726203-ellen"><img alt="Ellen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226546692p2/726203.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52491810?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellen" href="/user/show/726203-ellen">Ellen</a>
      </span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9853382820928640580">Great, great book. I've mentioned recently in reviews that I've read some time travel books people have recommended as "as good as Connie Willis" and they never turn out to be; this is the only other book I can think of that I put on the same level as <i>Doomsday Book</i> and <i>To Say Nothing of the Dog</i> (though I read it first, so really, it was more like reading <i>Doomsday Book</i> and thinking it was as good as <i>Pastwatch</i>). Highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_22990287" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22990287" />
    <a title="Bruce Sanders" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/271043-bruce-sanders"><img alt="Bruce Sanders" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22990287?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruce Sanders" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruce Sanders" href="/user/show/271043-bruce-sanders">Bruce Sanders</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22990287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8384378713112790971">This was recommended to me by a friend. It is an alternative history book rather than what I'd call a sci-fi book and I'm not really an alternative history fan. Pastwatch is an organization that does what it says. Via special machines Pastwatch personnel are able to tap into the past and watch history. One day one of the workers discovers that it may be possible to interact with the past and thus change the past (and everything in the future that follows from that change). It is later discovered</span>
  <span id="freeText8384378713112790971" style="display:none">This was recommended to me by a friend. It is an alternative history book rather than what I'd call a sci-fi book and I'm not really an alternative history fan. Pastwatch is an organization that does what it says. Via special machines Pastwatch personnel are able to tap into the past and watch history. One day one of the workers discovers that it may be possible to interact with the past and thus change the past (and everything in the future that follows from that change). It is later discovered that the past has been changed. Eventually, it is decided to change the past and destroy the present in order to make the world better by altering the outcome of Columbus' voyage to the New World. The reason it is decided to change the past is it is clear that in the present mankind has ruined the world and is headed for extinction. Interspersed with the chapters about Pastwatch are chapters concerning Columbus' attempts to obtain ships for a voyage West. These chapters are really an historical novel. They are quite fanciful but very well done and in many ways are the best part of the book. Two thirds of the way through the book the attempt to change the past is undertaken. The plot is quite interesting and cleverly done, but all in all it struck me as naive (both as to whether or not history would change as planned and whether that plan is actually any good to begin with). Let me put it this way without giving anything away. You learn at the end of the book that Columbus has fathered nine children. No matter how things have improved in other respects, that doesn't strike me as a strategy for avoiding the ruination of the planet in the future. All in all this book held my interest, but that is about it. There was nothing really profound about it and no great speculations put forth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8384378713112790971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1406954188" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1406954188" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8374007-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568348701p2/8374007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1406954188?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/8374007-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1406954188" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6186307872068006054">This book is 280 pages of people talking about doing things and only 60 pages of people actually doing those things that they already spent 280 pages talking about doing. Characters are introduced and then never really utilized. Characters that are utilized frequently defer to and reference characters that barely made an impression at the beginning of the book. In general, it's just not a well written story.<br/><br/>As far as the philosophy behind it, it's a pedantic story with the ending message, "Chris</span>
  <span id="freeText6186307872068006054" style="display:none">This book is 280 pages of people talking about doing things and only 60 pages of people actually doing those things that they already spent 280 pages talking about doing. Characters are introduced and then never really utilized. Characters that are utilized frequently defer to and reference characters that barely made an impression at the beginning of the book. In general, it's just not a well written story.<br /><br />As far as the philosophy behind it, it's a pedantic story with the ending message, "Christopher Columbus, slaver/rapist/murderer, had his heart in the right place. He was just a victim of his own time and circumstance." A weird moral that belongs next to theories like, "Could Hitler have been a great artist instead?" in the big book of, "Things we don't need to waste our time thinking about." Some times people do good things, some times people do bad things. And when people do really really bad things, I don't think we need to waste our time thinking, "But what if more people would've just helped them do more good things?" Don't waste your time reading the false redemption of one of history's villains, find a book praising one of history's many unsung heroes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6186307872068006054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_89477309" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89477309" />
    <a title="Claude Bertout" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2210843-claude-bertout"><img alt="Claude Bertout" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442486965p2/2210843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89477309?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claude Bertout" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claude Bertout" href="/user/show/2210843-claude-bertout">Claude Bertout</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89477309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10718102875828888064">Recommended by Jocelyn and Joje. It's a science-fiction, utopian novel as well as a thoughtful and well-researched reflection on History and its twists, a moral tale filled with lovely, compassionate and clever characters. At some point in my reading, I thought that the story was lacking a major villain (there is a minor one) to make the plot even more exciting and a little less heavy on the politically correct, but this may have been my wicked mind speaking. In the end, I came to realize that a</span>
  <span id="freeText10718102875828888064" style="display:none">Recommended by Jocelyn and Joje. It's a science-fiction, utopian novel as well as a thoughtful and well-researched reflection on History and its twists, a moral tale filled with lovely, compassionate and clever characters. At some point in my reading, I thought that the story was lacking a major villain (there is a minor one) to make the plot even more exciting and a little less heavy on the politically correct, but this may have been my wicked mind speaking. In the end, I came to realize that a villain was not really needed and would probably have made things more difficult for Card, who chose to stay close to the historical facts. Whether the change that the characters are plotting will succeed or not provides enough suspense, even though it does not make for a truly compulsive reading. In any case, it's an intriguing book and definitely worth reading if you enjoy historical novels or science fiction. It's the first time that I approach O.S. Card's work but certainly not the last.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10718102875828888064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4016387156452738838">4.5 stars! I ended up really loving this book. It is a favorite of people I love and trust but who have very different reading styles than I do, so it took some discipline for me to start it and pursue to the end. I would say about halfway in I was hooked, not only by the plan to alter the past, but also by the intimate look into Christopher Columbus’ world and the unquestioned assumptions that drove the people of his time. It is clear that Card immersed himself deeply in the history and culture</span>
  <span id="freeText4016387156452738838" style="display:none">4.5 stars! I ended up really loving this book. It is a favorite of people I love and trust but who have very different reading styles than I do, so it took some discipline for me to start it and pursue to the end. I would say about halfway in I was hooked, not only by the plan to alter the past, but also by the intimate look into Christopher Columbus’ world and the unquestioned assumptions that drove the people of his time. It is clear that Card immersed himself deeply in the history and culture and world of Columbus’ voyage before (and while) writing this book and the ideas he puts forth and questions he poses are intriguing. I love reading a book like this that has so much creative thinking in it that it spills over into other things I am thinking about and gives me a fresh perspective on many things beyond the scope of the book itself. Excellent!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4016387156452738838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_618226153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13245491-sarah-hipple?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer618226153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13826659775524582099">Pastwatch is a really interesting alternate history by Orson Scott Card.<br/><br/>It is really hard to give a summary of this book without giving away any spoilers. Normally I'd give a short summary and then go into my opinions, but I don't feel comfortable giving a summary here because one of the most important plot points isn't something you discover until half way through the book, and I'm not going to ruin that for you guys.<br/><br/>One of the most interesting parts of this book is that it takes place during t</span>
  <span id="freeText13826659775524582099" style="display:none">Pastwatch is a really interesting alternate history by Orson Scott Card.<br /><br />It is really hard to give a summary of this book without giving away any spoilers. Normally I'd give a short summary and then go into my opinions, but I don't feel comfortable giving a summary here because one of the most important plot points isn't something you discover until half way through the book, and I'm not going to ruin that for you guys.<br /><br />One of the most interesting parts of this book is that it takes place during two different time periods, and neither of them is ours. One is far off into the future. The other is during the time of Christopher Columbus.<br /><br />Card interweaves these two times and his many different characters masterfully. My favorite parts took place in the future. I likes those characters more than the characters in the time of Columbus. Card creates many sympathetic characters, but its pretty obvious that the real star of the story is the story itself, not any of the people in his story.<br /><br />Without spoilers, I'd like to say that the ending was pretty satisfying, a little too neat, and very bittersweet (for me at least).<br /><br />This book was impressive to read. Card brought together piece after piece after piece and somehow assembled them all into a pretty fascinating tale.<br /><br />The story is a bit dry at times, and, towards the end, Card's obvious belief in the good and importance of Christianity definitely annoyed me, but it was worth the read. This is one of those books that leaves you thinking, "What if . . . ?"<br /><br />I was wavering between three and four stars, but because I'm sure I'll be pondering over Card's ideas for months to come, I decided on four stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13826659775524582099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer221383620" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15279584999129013716">“I guess every writer who considers writing fiction occasionally has the experience of running across a book whose plot is one he was working on himself, thinking it an original idea.<br/><br/>I have had an idea for a SF novel very much along the lines of Pastwatch for at least 10 years. I never had the drive to bring the idea to life, just some sketches and development ideas. So I was shocked when I read Pastwatch. I realized how much of what we think is our own idea is just floating out there in the Zei</span>
  <span id="freeText15279584999129013716" style="display:none">“I guess every writer who considers writing fiction occasionally has the experience of running across a book whose plot is one he was working on himself, thinking it an original idea.<br /><br />I have had an idea for a SF novel very much along the lines of Pastwatch for at least 10 years. I never had the drive to bring the idea to life, just some sketches and development ideas. So I was shocked when I read Pastwatch. I realized how much of what we think is our own idea is just floating out there in the Zeitgeist for any of us to pick up.<br /><br />That issue aside I found Pastwatch fascinating. Like a lot of SF, I thought it was stronger on ideas than on psychology, I found the characters thin on the whole, but the ideas were well thought out and the history, very well thought out. Alternative history, especially the time travel variant, is very difficult to pull off well. "Guns of the South" and the "Axis of Time" trilogy are the two most interesting full length novels of this genre I've read in the last ten years and Pastwatch is at least as good.<br /><br />There is one issue that Pastwatch didn't take up that is central to my treatment and that is ...<br /><br />I think I'll keep that a secret in case I ever write my book.<br /><br />(2011) Turns out Arthur C. Clarke wrote a book that was EXACTLY the idea I thought was mine. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15279584999129013716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3291212651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3291212651" />
    <a title="Aberdeen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64266205-aberdeen"><img alt="Aberdeen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485372539p2/64266205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3291212651?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aberdeen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aberdeen" href="/user/show/64266205-aberdeen">Aberdeen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64266205-aberdeen?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64266205-aberdeen?shelf=blow-my-mind">blow-my-mind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64266205-aberdeen?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64266205-aberdeen?shelf=fantasy-in-our-world">fantasy-in-our-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64266205-aberdeen?shelf=not-just-white-people">not-just-white-people</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3291212651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18040521520452917501"><b>A lot of my thoughts on this are spoilery, but here’s the tl;dr version: I loved this book. Its driving force is ideas, which is right up my alley. It was the perfect blend of being grounded in historical detail but full of idealism and hypotheticals (what science fiction is for). It had a fascinating world-building (both historical and in the far future), characters I cared about, and ethical dilemmas.</b> Now for more in-depth thoughts and critiques of other people’s critiques … <br/><br/>Another reviewer s</span>
  <span id="freeText18040521520452917501" style="display:none"><b>A lot of my thoughts on this are spoilery, but here’s the tl;dr version: I loved this book. Its driving force is ideas, which is right up my alley. It was the perfect blend of being grounded in historical detail but full of idealism and hypotheticals (what science fiction is for). It had a fascinating world-building (both historical and in the far future), characters I cared about, and ethical dilemmas.</b> Now for more in-depth thoughts and critiques of other people’s critiques … <br /><br />Another reviewer said this is an ideas book rather than a character book. I agree that the ideas are the main propellers (which is personally right up my alley, although I completely understand that it isn't for everyone) but the characters also felt very real and sympathetic to me. Card did an admirable job making each of them unique and fleshed-out despite relatively small screen time (due to the multi-generational nature of the story, as well as the emphasis on ideas) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(example: that scene with Diko and Hunahpu at the very end? It gave me an ache in my chest, which shows me that I really cared about them.)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />Because the ideas are the main point, the ending wasn't as overdone as it otherwise would have felt. In a pure novel, it would have felt preachy <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[or too perfect. This may be a weird comparison, but it reminds me of the end of Jane Austen's Northanger Abbey, which I didn't like because it was too obvious and perfect <i>for a novel<i>, for the kind of book it is.</i></i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It is the culmination of the idea, a thought experiment. <b>This whole book is a thought experiment,</b> and a lot of the negative reviews I read stemmed from the fact that people either didn't understand or like that. It’s fine if you don't enjoy that kind of book, but don't complain that it isn't historically accurate (duh—it’s a what-might-have-been) or an info dump (it’s science fiction!). <br /><br />I <i>love</i> all the ethical dilemmas this book made me wrestle with <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[question for those who read it: would it have been right to go back if humanity had actually been living in a paradise, if they hadn't found out that it was all doomed anyway? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I love the interesting take on time travel and the fact that I could actually understand it. I love how it exposed me to cultures and a time period (central America and the Caribbean during the time of Columbus) that I know next to nothing about. Card’s list of resources at the end is impressive, how he did a lot of research to write this science fiction book (again! It's science-fiction! It's <i>not</i> historical fiction. It's just supposed to be grounded <i>enough</i> in what happened to provide a possible basis for another outcome). <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Also, I liked the ending. It was far more hopeful and positive than I expected. It didn't doom humanity again, and it didn't make Columbus the ultimate villain. It truly was his redemption. I'm sure this makes it very not-PC and will ruffle people’s feathers, but then, Card’s argument is also that the Mesoamerican people could've been formidable foes and that colonization was the greatest mistake of history, so it's not like he was being a white supremacist like some people claim. Of course, I am white, so I'd appreciate what from Central America or the Caribbean thinks about the story. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Last thing: I'm a Christian. Of course I don't agree with the way Christianity has often been used historically, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[nor do I agree with the new doctrines that the time travelers introduce  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <b>But I appreciate that Card highlighted the importance that religion plays in creating societies, unifying (and dividing) cultures, and inspiring people unlike anything else can.</b> It's something our modern culture often overlooks. And Christianity really is the best source for hope and healing (although it looks rather the opposite of how it's used in this book: not as a political pawn but as a transformation of individuals). <br /><br />Fascinating, too, the thought that the Central Americans were more Christian than the Europeans. I absolutely agree that they acted far more Christian than many Europeans (Card did a good job balancing that view with a condom nation of human sacrifice). My only caveat to that is that true Christianity is about far more than our actions—you can act like a good person and still be far from a relationship with God.<br /><br />All that to say, this book really made me think, for which I love it.<br /><br />Also Card's writing style really reminds me of Brandon Sanderson's, which is quite an endorsement.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18040521520452917501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2167747229" />
    <a title="Ellen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3166143-ellen"><img alt="Ellen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470073908p2/3166143.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2167747229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13824166298642322391">Outside of Ender's Game I'm not a big fan of Scott Card's work -- the few I've tried to read have put me to sleep. So, on that front this is a rousing success. Essentially a book about time travel, humans in the future have the ability to look back on all of human history, and realize that they can go back and reshape that history to undo all manner of evil. I don't think Scott Card fully understands how this world works, though; the explanation of how Pastwatch functions isn't real clear, and w</span>
  <span id="freeText13824166298642322391" style="display:none">Outside of Ender's Game I'm not a big fan of Scott Card's work -- the few I've tried to read have put me to sleep. So, on that front this is a rousing success. Essentially a book about time travel, humans in the future have the ability to look back on all of human history, and realize that they can go back and reshape that history to undo all manner of evil. I don't think Scott Card fully understands how this world works, though; the explanation of how Pastwatch functions isn't real clear, and when it comes to sending people back in time FOR THE FIRST TIME IN THIS HISTORY, they are inexplicably fully equipped to do this, understand perfectly how it will work...despite never having done it before. The characters aren't fully drawn and every woman and man who work together fall in love and get married, or at least get married. Scott Card has some pretty horrific beliefs, and so it would not surprise me if he ascribes to the Pence rule of never meeting with a woman alone; this book, anyway, doesn't suggest it's possible for a man to work alongside a woman without romance ensuing. <br /><br />In any case, it's sort of a fun look at reshaping history that takes forever to get to its point, and we spend far longer wandering around after Christopher Columbus than I'd like. I'm never quite clear on why everyone is so convinced of him as the man with the power to be the vessel through which they reshape history. Converting the world to Christianity is key to Scott Card's better version of the world, and sometimes his vision of the world really comes through in astounding ways. One of the Pastwatchers, Hunahpu, is a a Mayan descendent and at one point says: "I can promise you that a world ruled by the Tlaxcalans or the Mexica -- or even the Maya, for that matter -- would never have given rise to the democratic and tolerant and scientific values that eventually emerged from European culture..." (191-192). Good lecture, Orson Scott Card. Read this for book club and doubt I would have bothered otherwise, but it was more readable than most of his work outside Ender's Game.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13824166298642322391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1323284082662583439">This is only the second Orson Scott Card book I’ve read. When it comes to sci-fi, I’m an <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7779.Arthur_C__Clarke' title='Arthur C. Clarke' rel='nofollow noopener'> Arthur C. Clarke</a> fan all the way, and don’t dabble in much else. As with <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/901.Ender_s_Game__Ender_s_Saga___1_' title='Ender's Game (Ender's Saga, #1) by Orson Scott Card' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ender’s Game</a>
</i> I came to <i>Pastwatch</i> via a friend’s recommendation. She had others of Card’s books she liked more, but I opted to try this one because of the subject material. I was curious to see how Card would depict Columbus, a prominent figure in Card’s (and my) native culture of Mormonism. <br/><br/>In short, I didn’t find the book especial</span>
  <span id="freeText1323284082662583439" style="display:none">This is only the second Orson Scott Card book I’ve read. When it comes to sci-fi, I’m an <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7779.Arthur_C__Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke" rel="nofollow noopener"> Arthur C. Clarke</a> fan all the way, and don’t dabble in much else. As with <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/901.Ender_s_Game__Ender_s_Saga___1_" title="Ender's Game (Ender's Saga, #1) by Orson Scott Card" rel="nofollow noopener">Ender’s Game</a>
</i> I came to <i>Pastwatch</i> via a friend’s recommendation. She had others of Card’s books she liked more, but I opted to try this one because of the subject material. I was curious to see how Card would depict Columbus, a prominent figure in Card’s (and my) native culture of Mormonism. <br /><br />In short, I didn’t find the book especially memorable. It was a bit less engaging than <i>Ender’s Game</i>, but easily kept my attention. I suppose what I enjoyed best was watching the various characters face tough decisions. As for Mr. Card’s depiction of Columbus? Blah, nothing earth-shattering to report. This is a good story using a well-worn sci-fi premise: humanity manipulating space and time for a noble end. If you’ve liked other Orson Scott Card, I recommend this one. <br /></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5916890497899425861">It became really good after the page 200 or so (of 344).<br/>I like alternative history, and the whole idea of the book, although I have my second thoughts about intervening with things the way it was done in this story - anything more said would be a spoiler.<br/>It is just that there is too much preparation for the main event, which preparation did not do much for me, not in the sense of getting familiar with the characters, and not in the sense of making me justify them in matters that I did not agree </span>
  <span id="freeText5916890497899425861" style="display:none">It became really good after the page 200 or so (of 344).<br />I like alternative history, and the whole idea of the book, although I have my second thoughts about intervening with things the way it was done in this story - anything more said would be a spoiler.<br />It is just that there is too much preparation for the main event, which preparation did not do much for me, not in the sense of getting familiar with the characters, and not in the sense of making me justify them in matters that I did not agree with them in the first place.</span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer538316040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15659531445898360867">Pastwatch was one of those books that got better and better as I read. The concept of being able to change history is incredibly fascinating, both from a physics time-travel point of view, and from a historical/philosophical context. The concept of "Pastwatch" - being able to scroll through history as if watching it on film (or microfilm, as it were, which is how I pictured it in the book) is both well-constructed and well-executed in the book. Definitely a recommended read for history buffs, sc</span>
  <span id="freeText15659531445898360867" style="display:none">Pastwatch was one of those books that got better and better as I read. The concept of being able to change history is incredibly fascinating, both from a physics time-travel point of view, and from a historical/philosophical context. The concept of "Pastwatch" - being able to scroll through history as if watching it on film (or microfilm, as it were, which is how I pictured it in the book) is both well-constructed and well-executed in the book. Definitely a recommended read for history buffs, sci-fi nerds, philosophers, and humanitarians.</span>
